            .ot-floating-button__front{background-image:url('https://cdn-ukwest.onetrust.com/logos/static/ot_persistent_cookie_icon.png')}</style></head><body class="oxy-ui pubs-ui ahtml-page"><!--Google Tag Manager (noscript)--><noscript><iframe src="https://www.googletagmanager.com/ns.html?id=GTM-5HVSFVCN" height="0" width="0" style="display:none;visibility:hidden"></iframe></noscript><!--End Google Tag Manager (noscript)--><div class="viewport autopad"><div class="pnl pnl--drop autopad"><span id="top"><div id="wrapper"><div class="left_head"><a class="simple" href="/"><img class="rsc-logo" border="0" src="/content/NewImages/royal-society-of-chemistry-logo.png" alt="Royal Society of Chemistry"></a><br><span class="btnContainer"><a class="btn btn--tiny btn--primary" target="_blank" title="Link to PDF version" href="/en/content/articlepdf/2023/cp/d2cp04752g">View&nbsp;PDF&nbsp;Version</a></span><span class="btnContainer"><a class="btn btn--tiny btn--nobg" title="Link to previous article (id:d2cp04877a)" href="/en/content/articlehtml/2023/cp/d2cp04877a" target="_BLANK">Previous&nbsp;Article</a></span><span class="btnContainer"><a class="btn btn--tiny btn--nobg" title="Link to next article (id:d2cp01080a)" href="/en/content/articlehtml/2023/cp/d2cp01080a" target="_BLANK">Next&nbsp;Article</a></span></div><div class="right_head"><div id="crossmark_container"><div id="crossmark-content"><a id="open-crossmark" href="#" data-target="crossmark" style="cursor: pointer;"><img style="max-width:100px" id="crossmark-logo" src="https://crossmark-cdn.crossref.org/widget/v2.0/logos/CROSSMARK_Color_square.svg" alt="Check for updates"></a><script src="https://crossmark-cdn.crossref.org/widget/v2.0/widget.js"></script></div></div>&nbsp;</div><div class="article_info"> DOI:&nbsp;<a target="_blank" title="Link to landing page via DOI" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/D2CP04752G">10.1039/D2CP04752G</a>
(Paper)
<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/1463-9084/1999">Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.</a></span>, 2023, <strong>25</strong>, 4340-4348</div><h1 id="sect177"><span class="title_heading">Biphenylene network as sodium ion battery anode material<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a></span></h1><p class="header_text">
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Xin-Wei 
            Chen
          
        
      </span>, 
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Zheng-Zhe 
            Lin
          
        
      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Zheng-Zhe&nbsp;Lin (orcid.org/0000-0001-7188-6257) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-7188-6257"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>*<span class="bold"> and </span>
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Xi-Mei 
            Li
          
        
      </span>
      <br><span class="italic">School of Physics, Xidian University, Xian 710071, China. E-mail: <a href="mailto:zzlin@xidian.edu.cn">zzlin@xidian.edu.cn</a></span>
    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      12th October 2022
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 29th December 2022</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 16th January 2023</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>Sodium ion batteries possess several advantages for large-scale energy storage, such as low cost and enhanced safety. However, graphite or other anode materials are not satisfactory because the large radius of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> hinders their embedding and removal in the charge and discharge processes. Recently, a biphenylene network (BPN), a two-dimensional (2D) carbon allotrope, has been synthesized. In this paper, we reveal the potential possibility of BPN as a Na storage material. The theoretical results indicate the advantages of BPN as a sodium battery anode. The maximum specific capacity (413 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) is larger than that of the graphite-Li system (372 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>). With low Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion barrier (&lt;0.6 eV) and small volume expansion in the charging process (26%), BPN presents superiority to the graphite-Na system. Our findings show new insights into Na storage in BPN and provide guidance for the use of a BPN anode in sodium ion batteries.</p></div><hr>
    
      
      <h2 id="sect216"><span class="a_heading">1. Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>As energy conversion and storage devices, the demand for rechargeable batteries is growing.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15</span></sup></a> Nowadays, rechargeable metal-ion batteries play an important role in the sustainable development of the global economy. Among common metal-ion batteries, Li-ion batteries are the dominating medium of energy storage and may promote the improvement of sustainable storage of intermittent energy sources. However, the booming development of electric vehicles and large-scale application of Li-ion batteries make people worry about Li reserves. In contrast, Na is abundant and cheap. Recently, the emergence of Na-ion batteries has been widely considered by researchers. The development of Na batteries with long cycle life and no memory effect has important strategic significance. The electrode production and battery assembly processes of Na batteries have no obvious difference from Li batteries. Although the energy density is lower than that of Li batteries, Na batteries are more advantageous for large-scale energy storage applications.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">However, the larger ionic radius is a basic problem of Na batteries. The difference in the ionic radius between Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> and Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> leads to the performance of the Na batteries being lower than that of Li batteries. We need to find new electrode materials suitable for Na batteries. Recent research has suggested a simple method for predicting the configuration of layered Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> oxides, whose effectiveness has been verified by experiments.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6</span></sup></a> This method provides theoretical guidance for the design and preparation of layered oxide cathodes for low cost and high performance Na batteries. For anodes, common materials include carbon, nano-carbon, alloy materials and metal oxides, <span class="italic">etc</span>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">79</span></sup></a> As one of the key materials, the anode material is the electrode for the oxidation reaction, which plays a decisive role in the charge and discharge efficiency, capacity density and other properties of metal-ion batteries. Among common anode materials, graphitic materials are present in almost all carbon classes, including microporous carbons,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a> porous carbon composites<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11,12</span></sup></a> and soft carbons containing hydrogen.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13</span></sup></a> Graphitic carbon is the most commonly used anode in Li batteries.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14,15</span></sup></a> However, with larger ionic radius, Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> can hardly be embedded in graphite. In current Na batteries, hard carbon is used for providing more Na storage sites.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16</span></sup></a> Recently, many new materials have also been developed rapidly.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17</span></sup></a> The utilization of newly discovered carbon allotropes in emerging energy applications has also been paid much attention.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1823</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">As a fundamental element on the earth, carbon possesses a unique ability to form a variety of complex structures. In addition to graphite and diamond, people have synthesized many new carbon allotropes, including fullerenes, carbon nanotubes and graphene. With the development of synthesis technology, a large number of carbon allotropes with sp bonds or spsp2 and spsp<small><sup>3</sup></small> combinations have been obtained.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2428</span></sup></a> Beyond that, theoretical predictions of new carbon crystalline phases, <span class="italic">e.g.</span> M-carbon,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit29"><sup><span class="sup_ref">29</span></sup></a> bct C<small><sub>4</sub></small> carbon,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> penta-graphene,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31</span></sup></a> BC14 penta-diamond<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32</span></sup></a> and BCO-C<small><sub>16</sub></small><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33</span></sup></a> have been proposed. Bafekry <span class="italic">et al.</span> found new forms of two-dimensional porous carbon nitride C<small><sub>6</sub></small>N<small><sub>7</sub></small> and C<small><sub>6</sub></small>N.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34,35</span></sup></a> Very recently, 4,4-dibromo-2,2,2,5,5,5-hexafluoro-1,1:4,1-terphenyl was first polymerized into well-aligned poly(2,5-difluoro-<span class="italic">para</span>-phenylene) chains, and then undergoes CC coupling by HF-zipping to form a biphenylene network (BPN).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a> This method opens the way to large-scale chemical synthesis of planar sp<small><sup>2</sup></small>-hybridized carbon allotropes other than graphene. BPN exhibits improved performance in the hydrogen evolution reaction over that of graphene.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37</span></sup></a> The BPN motif not only acts as a spacer in a variety of functional molecules but also serves as a backbone for catalysts and ligands.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a> Recently, Han <span class="italic">et al.</span> investigated the possibility of using a BPN monolayer as a Na battery anode.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39</span></sup></a> Since the chemical synthesis of BPN is expected to be used in industrial production, the use of bulk BPN in Na battery anodes is worth studying.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">In this paper, we investigate the structural and electronic properties of BPN, and explore the possibility of BPN serving as a Na storage material and Na battery anode <span class="italic">via</span> density functional theory (DFT) calculations. The convex hull of the formation energy spectrum is employed to understand the Na intercalation process in BPN. The open-circuit voltage (OCV) indicates that BPN has a larger specific capacity (413 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) than the graphite-Li system. The Na diffusion behavior is studied to reveal the advantages of BPN as a Na storage material. Finally, the change in BPN volume during the Na charging process, as well as the change of interlayer distance, is found to be lower than that of the graphite-Na system. We expect that our results will contribute to the implementation of BPN as a Na storage material and Na battery anode in the future.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect262"><span class="a_heading">2. Computational methods</span></h2>
      <span>DFT calculations are performed <span class="italic">via</span> the projector augmented wave (PAW) method<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40,41</span></sup></a> and generalized gradient approximation with the PerdewBurkeErnzerhof (PBE) functional<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42</span></sup></a> as implemented in the Vienna <span class="italic">ab initio</span> simulation package (VASP).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4346</span></sup></a> The DFT-D3 method with BeckeJohnson damping<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47,48</span></sup></a> is selected as the correction of van der Waals interactions. To verify the key calculation results, the hybrid HSE06 functional<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit49"><sup><span class="sup_ref">49,50</span></sup></a> is applied to the BPNNa systems (see Section S1 for details, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). A kinetic energy cut-off of 500 eV for the plane wave expansion is set. The <span class="italic"></span>-centered MonkhorstPack method was used to sample the Brillouin zone with a spacing of 0.025 <small><sup>1</sup></small> for structural optimization. The convergence of total energy is considered to be achieved when the total energy difference between two iterated steps is less than 10<small><sup>5</sup></small> eV. To obtain optimized structures, total HellmannFeynman forces are reduced to 10<small><sup>3</sup></small> eV <small><sup>1</sup></small>. For the BPN monolayer, we set a vacuum space of 17  between sheets to prevent interaction between the replicas. Molecular dynamics (MD) simulations are performed at 300 K to verify the structure stability. The time step is set to 1 fs. With a pre-equilibrium of 2 ps, the simulations last 4 ps. The climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit51"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5153</span></sup></a> is employed to find the minimum energy paths and migration barriers of Na in BPN. Crystal orbital Hamilton population (COHP) analysis, which can well reflect the change of the crystal orbital,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit54"><sup><span class="sup_ref">54</span></sup></a> is carried out with the LOBSTER package.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit55"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5558</span></sup></a> The pbeVaspFit2015 basis is used with C(2s, 2p) and Na(3s) basis functions.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">To evaluate the stability of different stacking patterns in bulk BPN, we calculate the binding energy of C atoms, which is defined as</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><span id="eqn1"><span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>b</sub></small> = [<span class="italic">E</span>(BPN)  <span class="italic">nE</span>(isolate C atom)]/<span class="italic">n</span></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">n</span> is the total number of C atoms in the unit cell, and <span class="italic">E</span>(BPN) and <span class="italic">E</span>(isolate C atom) are the total energies of a BPN cell and an isolated C atom, respectively. Under this definition, the bulk structure with minimum <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>b</sub></small> indicates that it is the most stable one. The strength of the BPNNa cohesive reaction in 3  3 and 2  2 supercells in stages I and II is evaluated by the average adsorption energy that can be defined as<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2"><span id="eqn2"><span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small> = [<span class="italic">E</span>(BPNNa<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small>)  <span class="italic">E</span>(BPN)  <span class="italic">nE</span>(Na)]/<span class="italic">n</span></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table>Here, <span class="italic">n</span> represents the number of Na atoms in the supercell. <span class="italic">E</span>(BPNNa<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small>), <span class="italic">E</span>(BPN) and <span class="italic">E</span>(Na) mean the total energies of the BPNNa phase, BPN and a Na atom in the bulk bcc phase, respectively.<p></p>
      <p class="otherpara">To estimate the stability of BPNNa phases with different concentrations, we use formation energy per unit cell. For the formula Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>C<small><sub>6</sub></small>, it is define as</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn3"><span id="eqn3"><span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> = <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>uc</sub></small>(BPNNa<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>)  <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>uc</sub></small>(BPN)  <span class="italic">xE</span>(Na)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(3)</td></tr></tbody></table>Here, <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>uc</sub></small>(BPNNa<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>) is the total energy of Na-intercalated BPN per unit cell (six C atoms). <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>uc</sub></small>(BPN) is the total energy of pure BPN unit cell. <span class="italic">x</span> is the Na number in Na-intercalated BPN per unit cell. The open-circuit voltage (OCV) between two different Na contents <span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> and <span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> in bulk BPN is calculated as<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn4"><a href="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-t1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp04752g-t1.tif" src="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-t1.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(4)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>uc(ground-state)</sub></small>(BPNNa<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>) is the total energy of Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>C<small><sub>6</sub></small> in the ground state per BPN unit cell.<p></p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect350"><span class="a_heading">3. Results and discussion</span></h2>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect353"><span class="b_heading">3.1 Structural, mechanical and electronic properties of BPN</span></h3>
        <span>We first obtain structural information of the BPN monolayer (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(a)</a>, with the primitive cell shown by red dashed lines). The rectangular primitive cell belongs to the 2D <span class="italic">Pmm</span> space group, composed of six C atoms on the same plane. The lattice constants of monolayer BPN are <span class="italic">a</span> = 4.52  and <span class="italic">b</span> = 3.77 . The C atoms with sp<small><sup>2</sup></small> hybridization constitute tetragonal, hexagonal, and octagonal rings. The CC bond lengths and angles in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(a)</a> are <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> = 1.45 , <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> = 1.41 , <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>3</sub></small> = 1.45 , <span class="italic"></span><small><sub>1</sub></small> = 114.7 and <span class="italic"></span><small><sub>2</sub></small> = 90.0. Bafekry <span class="italic">et al.</span> verified the dynamical stability of the BPN monolayer <span class="italic">via</span> phonon band dispersion and determined a large proportion of strong covalent bonds between C atoms using the electron localization function (ELF).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit59"><sup><span class="sup_ref">59</span></sup></a> The binding energy per C atom (calculated by <a title="" href="#eqn1">eqn (1)</a>) is 7.52 eV per atom, which is higher than that of graphene (8.06 eV per atom) and diamond (8.05 eV per atom) but lower than that of graphdiyne (7.26 eV per atom).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a> This means that the BPN monolayer is stable in energetics.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp04752g-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The structure of the BPN monolayer. The unit cell is shown by dashed lines. (b) The energy bands, PDOS and COHP between the nearest CC atoms of the BPN monolayer. (c) The structure of bulk BPN. (d) The energy bands, PDOS and COHP between the nearest CC atoms of bulk BPN. (e) The ELF in bulk BPN. The left shows the C layer and the right shows the plane between the C layers. (f) The phonon spectrum of bulk BPN.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The structure of bulk BPN is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(c)</a>. The lattice constants of bulk BPN are <span class="italic">a</span> = 4.50 , <span class="italic">b</span> = 3.77  and <span class="italic">c</span> = 6.29 . The bulk phase is composed of BPN monolayer stacking. The binding energy of BPN bulk (7.64 eV per atom) is lower than that of the BPN monolayer and graphdiyne, which indicates that bulk BPN is also stable. The bulk lattice belongs to the <span class="italic">Pmma</span> space group (orthorhombic). The ELF is used to evaluate the characteristics of the bonding properties. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(e)</a> shows the ELF in the (001) plane. The ELF value between C atomic layers is almost zero. Similar to graphite, the C layers are bound <span class="italic">via</span> van der Waals interactions without covalent bonding. The dynamical stability of bulk BPN is verified <span class="italic">via</span> phonon band dispersion as well as MD simulations. The phonon spectra of the most stable AB pattern (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(f)</a>) have no apparent imaginary frequencies but U-shaped negative values near the <span class="italic"></span> point because of the flexural acoustic mode but not instability, which can be seen in the phonon bands of many other 2D materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit60"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6062</span></sup></a></p>
        <p class="otherpara">In order to understand the electronic properties, we obtain the band structures, the projected density of states (PDOS) and COHP of monolayer (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(b)</a>) and bulk BPN (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(d)</a>). To verify the results, we also employ the hybrid HSE06 functional to show that the calculated bands are similar to the PBE results (see Section S1, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). Consistent with the experimental result,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a> the bands crossing the Fermi level indicate that BPN is metallic. A tiled Dirac cone (0.53/0.52 eV above the Fermi level of BPN monolayer/bulk, respectively) can be found in the BPN band structures (denoted by the red circle in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(b) and (d)</a>), the formation of which is attributed to the out-of-plane p<small><sub>z</sub></small> orbitals. It also exists in other 2D materials like penta-NiSb<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayer<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit63"><sup><span class="sup_ref">63</span></sup></a> and Be<small><sub>5</sub></small>C<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit64"><sup><span class="sup_ref">64</span></sup></a> According to the PDOS, the C atoms have sp<small><sup>2</sup></small> hybridization because the low-energy bands mix 2s, 2p<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small> and 2p<small><sub><span class="italic">y</span></sub></small> orbitals. The BPN bands near the Fermi level are mainly composed of out-of-plane 2p<small><sub><span class="italic">z</span></sub></small> orbitals, which bring BPN metallic characteristics. To observe the CC bonding more clearly, we focus on the COHP between the atomic orbitals on the nearest CC atoms. Both monolayer and bulk BPN show negative COHP values (<span class="italic">i.e.</span> bonding) below the Fermi level, and positive COHP values (<span class="italic">i.e.</span> anti-bonding) above the Fermi level. The situation is similar to that of graphite (see Section S4, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), which indicates perfect CC bonding in BPN.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">For bulk BPN, we consider AA and several AB patterns as possible stacking (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2</a>). The binding energies of C atoms are listed in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>. Among these structures, the most stable one is AB stacking (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2(b)</a>). The intralayer CC bond lengths and angles have no obvious difference with single layer BPN. The interlayer distance <span class="italic">d</span> = 3.15  is close to that of graphite (3.3 ) (the interlayer distance of AA stacking is 3.35 ). In this structure, one layer is slipped by 0.20  along the <span class="italic">x</span> direction relative to another layer. MD simulations for the AA and AB patterns show that both structures can be sustained during the simulation time (for the details, see Section S3, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). So, the stability of BPN bulk is confirmed by the above results.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp04752g-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">Possible (a) AA and (bf) AB stacking of BPN. Every unit cell includes two BPN layers. The structure in (b) is with slippage along the <span class="italic">x</span> direction. The structure in (e) is with slippage along the <span class="italic">y</span> direction. The structure in (c) is with slippage along both the <span class="italic">x</span> and <span class="italic">y</span> directions. In (d), the octagonal rings in a layer pile on the square rings of another layer. In (f), the hexagonal rings in a layer pile on the square rings of another layer.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>

		</div>

          <hr class="hrule"><br>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect459"><span class="b_heading">3.2 Na intercalation in BPN</span></h3>
        <span>For the BPN monolayer, the formation energy <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> = +0.14 eV of NaC<small><sub>6</sub></small>, which is larger than zero, is disadvantageous to Na adsorption (see Section. S2, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). In this section, we reveal the potential ability of BPN bulk as a stable Na battery anode. We primarily investigate the intercalation of Na atoms. In the charging process, the intercalation of Na occurs in stages, which is considered to be similar to the case of the graphite anode.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit65"><sup><span class="sup_ref">65</span></sup></a> For example, in stage I (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(a)</a>), every interlayer spacing is uniformly filled by Na atoms. In stage II (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(a)</a>), Na atoms alternatively fill into one interlayer spacing, leaving another interlayer spacing empty and in stage <span class="italic">n</span>, Na atoms enter one layer while leaving <span class="italic">n</span>  1 layers empty between each Na-intercalated layer. We choose several possible configurations of stage I with Na atoms located on the oct-rings, hex-rings, tetra-rings, and on both oct- and hex-rings (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(b)</a>). We use average adsorption energy <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small> to determine the most stable adsorption site. The average Na adsorption energies are listed in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab2">Table 2</a>, which indicate that the oct-ring is the perfect site. To verify the stability of the BPNNa systems, MD simulations are performed (see Section S3, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(c) and (d)</a> show the COHP of CNa atom pairs in NaC<small><sub>6</sub></small> (stage I) and NaC<small><sub>12</sub></small> (stage II), respectively. We can see that the COHP between C and Na atoms is weak, indicating that no effective bonding exists between C and Na. The CNa interactions are just physical adsorption.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp04752g-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Stages I and II of Na intercalation. Brown and yellow balls represent C and Na atoms, respectively. (b) Several Na adsorption configurations in stage I. The red dashed lines present periodic boundary conditions. (c) COHP of CNa in stage I. (d) COHP of CNa in stage II.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        
<div class="table_caption"><b>Table 2</b> <span id="tab2">The average adsorption energies of Na on different sites of stage I</span>
		</div>

            <div class="rtable__wrapper"><div class="rtable__inner" style="min-width: 433px;"><table class="tgroup rtable" border="0"><colgroup><col><col><col><col><col></colgroup>
              
              
              
              
              
              <thead align="left">
                <tr align="left" valign="bottom">
                  <th align="left" valign="bottom" class="border_black">Site</th>

                  <th align="left" valign="bottom" class="border_black">Tetra-ring</th>
                  <th align="left" valign="bottom" class="border_black">Oct- and hex-ring</th>
                </tr>
              </thead>
              <tbody align="left">
                <tr align="left">
                  <td valign="top">
                    <span class="italic">E</span>
                    <small><sub>ad</sub></small> (eV atom<small><sup>1</sup></small>)</td>
                  <td valign="top">0.66</td>
                  <td valign="top">0.47</td>
                  <td valign="top">0.46</td>
                  <td valign="top">0.17</td>
                </tr>
              </tbody>
            </table></div></div>
          <hr class="hrule"><br>
        <p class="otherpara">In the case of Li-intercalated graphite, when the Li concentration increases, it is transformed from higher stages to lower stages.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit53"><sup><span class="sup_ref">53</span></sup></a> As Li atoms are intercalated, the hybridization between the Li valence electrons and graphite interlayer states perturbs and screens the CC van der Waals bonds, which causes the change from AB stacking of graphite to AA stacking. So, we anticipate the situation could occur in Na-intercalated BPN as well. In stage II of Na-intercalated BPN (with lower Na concentration than stage I), the layers filled with Na present AA stacking, while the empty layers present AB stacking (the right panel in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(a)</a>). In stage I with higher Na concentration, all the stacking transforms to AA. Similar to the graphite-Li system that possesses AB stacking at low Li concentration and AA stacking at high Li concentration,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit66"><sup><span class="sup_ref">66</span></sup></a> Na-intercalated BPN exhibits the same characteristics (see <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(d)</a>).</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp04752g-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The average adsorption energies <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small> changing with Na concentration <span class="italic">x</span> in bulk BPN. (b) The formation energies <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> and the convex hull changing with Na concentration <span class="italic">x</span> in bulk BPN. (c) OCV profiles as a function of Na concentration <span class="italic">x</span>. (d) Several typical structures of BPNNa phases. Brown and yellow balls represent C and Na atoms, respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect522"><span class="b_heading">3.3 Convex hull and anode potential of Na-intercalated BPN</span></h3>
        <span>To evaluate the thermodynamic evolution of Na-intercalated BPN with different Na concentrations, we search for possible configurations in the 3  3 and 2  2 Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>C<small><sub>6</sub></small> supercell (0 &lt; <span class="italic">x</span>  2) in stages I and II. Here, <span class="italic">x</span> &gt; 1 denotes the BPN primitive cell excessively filled with more than one Na. The average adsorption energies (<a title="" href="#eqn2">eqn (2)</a>) of 164 non-identical configurations (Several typical structures are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(d)</a>) are obtained to determine the ordered ground states (see <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(a)</a>). We also obtain formation energies per unit cell (<a title="" href="#eqn3">eqn (3)</a>) of these configurations (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(b)</a>).</span>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(a)</a> shows that when <span class="italic">x</span> &gt; 0.56, stage I is more favorable to form. But when <span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 0.56, stage II gets more dominant. Although we have only considered the Na-intercalated BPN in stages I and II, higher stages may exist with low enough Na concentration. As Na atoms are intercalated, the interlayer distance of BPN increases, which is advantageous for more Na atoms to intercalate. The average adsorption energy <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small> decreases with the Na number until the concentration reaches <span class="italic">x</span> = 0.889 (see <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(a)</a>). This means that when <span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 0.889, the increasing interlayer distance brings the aggregated Na atoms lower energy. But with more Na atoms (<span class="italic">x</span> &gt; 0.889), the interlayer distance increases slowly and the repulsion between Na atoms increases, leading to a higher <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small>. According to the formation energies <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(b)</a>, as the Na concentration reaches <span class="italic">x</span> = 1.111, the repulsion holds a dominant position, ultimately leading to a lowest <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> (<span class="italic">i.e.</span> the ground state, see <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(d)</a>). With higher Na concentration (<span class="italic">x</span> &gt; 1.111), the system becomes less stable because of loading excess Na atoms.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">To evaluate the OCVs in the discharge process, we pay attention to the low-energy configurations in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(b)</a>. The formation energy <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> of Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>C<small><sub>6</sub></small> represents the total energy decrease in the formation of CNa composites. In low Na concentration, stage II is more stable than stage I. It should be pointed out that in the actual discharge process, the OCVs might be related to extra stages that we have not calculated here. Generally, in low Na concentration, the formation of higher stages may be attributed to the ground state configurations. Here, average Na intercalation potential is used to describe the potential of the anode. According to the convex hull of <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> (the outline in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(b)</a>), we calculate the OCVs of the BPN anode <span class="italic">via</span><a title="" href="#eqn4">eqn (4)</a>, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(c)</a>. OCV is the average Na atom intercalation potential (relative to Na bulk) between two different phases. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(c)</a>, the OCVs are divided into 7 platforms, the values of which are 0.68, 0.42, 0.14, 0.17, 0.40 and 0.46 V. With increasing <span class="italic">x</span>, the value of OCV decreases. It is noteworthy that OCV &lt; 0 can cause the formation of dendrites<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit67"><sup><span class="sup_ref">67</span></sup></a> in BPNNa. Thus, the maximum appropriate Na concentration in bulk BPN is Na<small><sub>1.111</sub></small>C<small><sub>6</sub></small>, and its specific capacity 413 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> is higher than that of the graphite-Li system (372 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit68"><sup><span class="sup_ref">68</span></sup></a> To verify the results, we employed the hybrid HSE06 functional to calculate the formation energies of key structures in the convex hull. The resulted tendencies of the convex hull and the OCV profile are coincident with that of the PBE functional (see Section S1, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The HSE06 calculations confirm the reliability of the above results.</p>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect579"><span class="b_heading">3.4 Na migration barriers in BPN</span></h3>
        <span>Good ion diffusion is also important for anode materials. It has a great influence on battery reaction rates. In this section, we pay attention to Na diffusion barriers in BPN. In BPN, oct-rings are the most stable sites for Na adsorption. So, we mainly explore the migration between oct-rings. We first investigate in-plane and cross-plane diffusions of Na on a 3  3 BPN monolayer. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(a)(c)</a> show the in-plane migration paths and the corresponding energy profiles. Path <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(a) and (b)</a> is the diffusion along the <span class="italic">x</span>/<span class="italic">y</span> direction, respectively. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(c)</a> presents a long-distance jump from one oct-ring to another with a hex-ring as an intermediate. Their migration barriers in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(a)(c)</a> are 0.22, 0.22 and 0.21 eV, respectively, which are all lower than the Li migration barriers 0.42, 0.44 and 0.43 eV on a BPN monolayer.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit69"><sup><span class="sup_ref">69</span></sup></a> For the cross-plane migration through the oct-ring hollow of the BPN monolayer, the barrier is very high (6.89 eV). This indicates that cross-plane migration is kinetically prohibited. As for the cross-plane migration of a Na atom through a double vacancy defect site of bilayer graphite, this is also a difficulty.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit70"><sup><span class="sup_ref">70</span></sup></a></span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp04752g-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">(ac) Show in-plane diffusion paths of Na on BPN monolayer and the energy curves. (df) Show in-plane diffusion paths of Na on bulk BPN and the energy curves. Brown and yellow balls represent C and Na atoms, respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Then, we investigated Na diffusion in bulk BPN. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(d)(f)</a> show possible Na diffusion paths and corresponding barriers in a 3  3  1 bulk BPN supercell. The migration barriers are 0.39, 0.55 and 0.37 eV, which are lower than the barriers in AB-stacked bilayer graphene with double vacancy defects.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit70"><sup><span class="sup_ref">70</span></sup></a> In general, BPN has periodically arranged tetra-rings, hex-rings and oct-rings. The hex-rings are similar to those in graphite, and the oct-rings are similar to the defect sites in graphite. The adsorption sites on the hex-rings bring lower adsorption energy for Na. Therefore, the migration barriers of Na in PBN are a bit higher than those in defect graphite.</p>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect596"><span class="b_heading">3.5 Volume and interlayer distance of bulk BPN</span></h3>
        <span>In this section, we investigate the changes in volume and interlayer distance of BPN under different Na concentrations. For a certain Na concentration <span class="italic">x</span>, the ground state configuration with minimum energy is studied. For <span class="italic">x</span> = 0  2, the interlayer distances and volumes are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6(a) and (b)</a>, respectively. We record the data in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab3">Table 3</a>. We can see that the change of volume is consistent with that of interlayer distance because Na intercalation nearly has little influence on the intralayer area. The change in volume is mainly attributed to the change in interlayer distance. It is worth noting that there is an abrupt change from Na<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>C<small><sub>6</sub></small> to Na<small><sub>0.556</sub></small>C<small><sub>6</sub></small>, which is attributed to the change of stage. The change rate of volume and interlayer distance from Na<small><sub>0</sub></small>C<small><sub>6</sub></small> to Na<small><sub>1.111</sub></small>C<small><sub>6</sub></small> reaches 26.3% and 24.1%, respectively, compared with the change rate of interlayer distance in graphite-Na phase from Na<small><sub>0</sub></small>C<small><sub>6</sub></small> to Na<small><sub>1</sub></small>C<small><sub>6</sub></small> of 55.9%.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit71"><sup><span class="sup_ref">71</span></sup></a> A good anode material requires a low volume change that determines the stability during cation intercalation. So, to this point, bulk BPN is more suitable as a Na anode storage material compared with graphite.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp04752g-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2023/CP/d2cp04752g/d2cp04752g-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The interlayer distance and (b) the volume of the unit cell of BPNNa in ground states as a function of <span class="italic">x</span>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>

		</div>

          <hr class="hrule"><br>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect730"><span class="a_heading">4. Summary</span></h2>
      <span>In summary, the structural and electronic properties of BPN are investigated <span class="italic">via</span> DFT calculations. The structure of BPN is composed of periodical four-, six-, and eight-membered carbon rings. The band structures and PDOS reveal its metallic electronic properties. We find the most stable AB stacking pattern of bulk BPN, whose dynamical stability is verified <span class="italic">via</span> phonon band dispersion. The ELF shows strong intralayer covalent bonding, while the layers are bound by van der Waals interactions. To comprehensively understand the characteristics of BPN for Na ion battery applications, we present the results for Na distribution in bulk BPN and the OCVs from the convex hull of formation energy. The sites in the oct-rings are most favorable to Na adsorption. As more Na intercalated in these sites, the stacking pattern of this material changes from AB to AA. The maximum of OCV reaches 0.68 V. The flat section of OCV indicates that BPNNa possesses a specific capacity of 413 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> that is higher than that of the graphite-Li battery. We also found that the migration barriers of Na in monolayer and bulk BPN remain low enough for ion diffusion. Finally, we investigate the changes in volume and interlayer distance during Na insertion. They are both smaller than those of the graphite-Na phase. We hope that our study will inspire new interest in the experimental realization of BPN on a large scale suitable for commercial production.</span>

            <br><a href="https://cookiepedia.co.uk/giving-consent-to-cookies" class="privacy-notice-link" rel="noopener" target="_blank" aria-label="More information about your privacy, opens in a new tab">More information</a></div><button id="accept-recommended-btn-handler">Allow all</button><section class="ot-sdk-row ot-cat-grp"><h3 id="ot-category-title"> Manage consent preferences</h3><div class="ot-pli-hdr ot-leg-border-color"><span class="ot-li-title">Consent</span> <span class="ot-li-title">Legitimate interest</span></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0001"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0001" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0001 ot-status-id-C0001"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0001">Strictly necessary cookies</h4></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0001">These cookies are necessary for the website to function and cannot be switched off in our systems. They are usually only set in response to actions made by you which amount to a request for services, such as setting your privacy preferences, logging in or filling in forms. You can set your browser to block or alert you about these cookies, but some parts of the site will not then work. These cookies do not store any personally identifiable information.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0002"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0002">Analytical cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0002" id="ot-group-id-C0002" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0002"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Analytical cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0002">These cookies allow us to count visits and traffic sources so we can measure and improve the performance of our site. They help us to know which pages are the most and least popular and see how visitors move around the site. All information these cookies collect is aggregated and therefore anonymous. If you do not allow these cookies we will not know when you have visited our site, and will not be able to monitor its performance.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0003"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0003">Functional cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0003" id="ot-group-id-C0003" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0003"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Functional cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0003">These cookies enable the website to provide enhanced functionality and personalisation. They may be set by us or by third party providers whose services we have added to our pages. If you do not allow these cookies then some or all of these services may not function properly.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0004"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0004" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0004"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0004">Targeting cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0004" id="ot-group-id-C0004" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0004" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0004"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0004"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Targeting cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0004">These cookies may be set through our site by our advertising partners.
